</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Labor" title="Template:Labor"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Labor" title="Template talk:Labor"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Labor&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>A <b>trade union</b> (<b>labor union</b> in <a href="/wiki/American_English" title="American English">American English</a>), often simply referred to as a <b>union</b>, is an organization of workers intent on "maintaining or improving the conditions of their <a href="/wiki/Employment" title="Employment">employment</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-webb_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-webb-1">[1]</a></sup> such as attaining better <a href="/wiki/Wage" title="Wage">wages</a> and benefits (such as holiday, health care, and retirement), improving working conditions, improving safety standards, establishing complaint procedures, developing rules governing status of employees (rules governing promotions, just-cause conditions for termination) and protecting the <a href="/wiki/Integrity" title="Integrity">integrity</a> of their trade through the increased <a href="/wiki/Bargaining_power" title="Bargaining power">bargaining power</a> wielded by solidarity among workers.
</p><p>Trade unions typically fund their head office and legal team functions through regularly imposed fees called union dues. The delegate staff of the trade union representation in the workforce are usually made up of workplace volunteers who are often appointed by members in democratic elections.
</p><p>The trade union, through an elected leadership and bargaining committee, bargains with the employer on behalf of its members, known as the <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/rank_and_file" title="wikt:rank and file">rank-and-file</a>, and negotiates <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Labour_and_employment_law" title="Labour and employment law">labour contracts</a> (collective bargaining agreements) with employers.
</p><p>Unions may organize a particular section of skilled or unskilled workers (<a href="/wiki/Craft_unionism" title="Craft unionism">craft unionism</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poole,_M._1986_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Poole,_M._1986-2">[2]</a></sup> a cross-section of workers from various trades (<a href="/wiki/General_union" title="General union">general unionism</a>), or an attempt to organize all workers within a particular industry (<a href="/wiki/Industrial_unionism" title="Industrial unionism">industrial unionism</a>). The agreements negotiated by a union are binding on the rank-and-file members and the employer, and in some cases on other non-member workers. Trade unions traditionally have a <a href="/wiki/Constitution" title="Constitution">constitution</a> which details the governance of their bargaining unit and also have governance at various levels of government depending on the industry that binds them legally to their negotiations and functioning.
</p><p>Originating in Great Britain, trade unions became popular in many countries during the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>. Trade unions may be composed of individual workers, <a href="/wiki/Professional" title="Professional">professionals</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retiree" title="Retiree">past workers</a>, students, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apprentice" title="Apprentice">apprentices</a> or the unemployed. Trade union density, or the percentage of workers belonging to a trade union, <a href="#Prevalence_worldwide">is highest in the Nordic countries</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ILO_Density_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-ILO_Density-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Garment_Workers_on_Strike,_New_York_City_circa_1913.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="460" data-file-width="650" decoding="async" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Garment_Workers_on_Strike%2C_New_York_City_circa_1913.jpg/250px-Garment_Workers_on_Strike%2C_New_York_City_circa_1913.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Garment_Workers_on_Strike%2C_New_York_City_circa_1913.jpg/375px-Garment_Workers_on_Strike%2C_New_York_City_circa_1913.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Garment_Workers_on_Strike%2C_New_York_City_circa_1913.jpg/500px-Garment_Workers_on_Strike%2C_New_York_City_circa_1913.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Garment_Workers_on_Strike,_New_York_City_circa_1913.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Garment workers on strike, New York City circa 1913</div></div></div>
<p>Since the publication of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Trade_Unionism" title="History of Trade Unionism">History of Trade Unionism</a></i> (1894) by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sidney_Webb" title="Sidney Webb">Sidney</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beatrice_Webb" title="Beatrice Webb">Beatrice Webb</a>, the predominant historical view is that a trade union "is a continuous association on wage earners for the purpose of maintaining or improving the conditions of their employment."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-webb_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-webb-1">[1]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a> described trade unions thus: "The value of labour -power constitutes the conscious and explicit foundation of the trade unions, whose importance for the ... working class can scarcely be overestimated. The trade unions aim at nothing less than to prevent the reduction of wages below the level that is traditionally maintained in the various branches of industry. That is to say, they wish to prevent the price of labour -power from falling below its value" (<i>Capital</i> V1, 1867, p. 1069). Early <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxists</a> also saw trade unions as a way to <a href="/wiki/Democratization" title="Democratization">democratise</a> the workplace. Through this democratisation, they argued, the capture of political power would be possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>A modern definition by the Australian Bureau of Statistics states that a trade union is "an organization consisting predominantly of employees, the principal activities of which include the negotiation of rates of pay and conditions of employment for its members."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Yet historian R. A. Lesson, in <i>United we Stand</i> (1971), said:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Two conflicting views of the trade-union movement strove for ascendancy in the nineteenth century: one the defensive-restrictive guild-craft tradition passed down through journeymen's clubs and friendly societies, ... the other the aggressive-expansionist drive to unite all 'labouring men and women' for a 'different order of things'.</p></blockquote>
<p>Recent historical research by Bob James in <i>Craft, Trade or Mystery</i> (2001) puts forward the view that trade unions are part of a broader movement of <a href="/wiki/Benefit_society" title="Benefit society">benefit societies</a>, which includes medieval guilds, <a href="/wiki/Freemasonry" title="Freemasonry">Freemasons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Independent_Order_of_Odd_Fellows" title="Independent Order of Odd Fellows">Oddfellows</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Friendly_Society" title="Friendly Society">friendly societies</a>, and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fraternal_organisation" title="Fraternal organisation">fraternal organizations</a>.
</p><p>The 18th-century <a href="/wiki/Economist" title="Economist">economist</a> <a href="/wiki/Adam_Smith" title="Adam Smith">Adam Smith</a> noted the imbalance in the rights of workers in regards to owners (or "masters"). In <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wealth_of_Nations" title="The Wealth of Nations">The Wealth of Nations</a></i>, <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Chapter_8:_Of_the_Wages_of_Labour" title="s:Chapter 8: Of the Wages of Labour">Book I, chapter 8</a>, Smith wrote:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>We rarely hear, it has been said, of the combination of masters, though frequently of those of workmen. But whoever imagines, upon this account, that masters rarely combine, is as ignorant of the world as of the subject. Masters are always and everywhere in a sort of tacit, but constant and uniform combination, not to raise the wages of labor above their actual rate[.]
When workers combine, masters ... never cease to call aloud for the assistance of the civil magistrate, and the rigorous execution of those laws which have been enacted with so much severity against the combination of servants, labourers and journeymen.</p></blockquote>
<p>As Smith noted, unions were illegal for many years in most countries, although Smith argued that it should remain illegal to fix wages or prices by employees or employers. There were severe penalties for attempting to organize unions, up to and including execution. Despite this, unions were formed and began to acquire <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Political_power" title="Political power">political power</a>, eventually resulting in a body of labour law that not only legalized organizing efforts, but codified the relationship between employers and those employees organized into unions.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Collegium_(ancient_Rome)" title="Collegium (ancient Rome)">Collegium (ancient Rome)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guild" title="Guild">Guild</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trade_Guilds">Trade Guilds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Trade Guilds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FrameBreaking-1812.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1045" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/FrameBreaking-1812.jpg/220px-FrameBreaking-1812.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/FrameBreaking-1812.jpg/330px-FrameBreaking-1812.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/FrameBreaking-1812.jpg/440px-FrameBreaking-1812.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FrameBreaking-1812.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Early 19th century workplace militancy manifested in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luddism" title="Luddism">Luddite riots</a>, when unemployed workers destroyed labour saving machines.</div></div></div><p>Following the unification of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_of_the_ancient_Near_East" title="List of cities of the ancient Near East">city-states</a> in <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumer</a> by <a href="/wiki/Sargon_of_Akkad" title="Sargon of Akkad">Sargon of Akkad</a> into a <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_Empire" title="Akkadian Empire">single empire</a> ruled from his <a href="/wiki/Akkad_(city)" title="Akkad (city)">home city</a> circa 2334 BC, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Mesopotamian_units_of_measurement" title="Ancient Mesopotamian units of measurement">common Mesopotamian standards</a> for <a href="/wiki/Length" title="Length">length</a>, <a href="/wiki/Area" title="Area">area</a>, <a href="/wiki/Volume" title="Volume">volume</a>, <a href="/wiki/Weight" title="Weight">weight</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Time" title="Time">time</a> used by <a href="/wiki/Artisan" title="Artisan">artisan</a> <a href="/wiki/Guild" title="Guild">guilds</a> in each city was promulgated by <a href="/wiki/Naram-Sin_of_Akkad" title="Naram-Sin of Akkad">Naram-Sin of Akkad</a> (c. 2254–2218 BC), Sargon's grandson, including for <a href="/wiki/Shekel" title="Shekel">shekels</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Powell_1995,_p.1955_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Powell_1995,_p.1955-7">[7]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Code_of_Hammurabi#Laws" title="Code of Hammurabi"><i>Codex Hammurabi</i> Law 234</a> (c. 1755–1750 BC) stipulated a 2-shekel <a href="/wiki/Prevailing_wage" title="Prevailing wage">prevailing wage</a> for each 60-<a href="/wiki/Ancient_Mesopotamian_units_of_measurement#Capacity_or_volume" title="Ancient Mesopotamian units of measurement">gur</a> (300-<a href="/wiki/Bushel" title="Bushel">bushel</a>) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merchant_vessel" title="Merchant vessel">vessel</a> constructed in an <a href="/wiki/Employment_contract" title="Employment contract">employment contract</a> between a <a href="/wiki/Shipbuilding" title="Shipbuilding">shipbuilder</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Ship-owner" title="Ship-owner">ship-owner</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sommer_1903_p._85_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sommer_1903_p._85-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harper_1904_p._85_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harper_1904_p._85-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King_1910_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-King_1910-10">[10]</a></sup> Law 275 stipulated a <a href="/wiki/Ferry" title="Ferry">ferry</a> <a href="/wiki/Price" title="Price">rate</a> of 3-<a href="/wiki/Gerah" title="Gerah">gerah</a> per day on a <a href="/wiki/Charterparty" title="Charterparty">charterparty</a> between a <a href="/wiki/Chartering_(shipping)" title="Chartering (shipping)">ship charterer</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Sea_captain" title="Sea captain">shipmaster</a>. Law 276 stipulated a 2<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1020198016">.mw-parser-output .frac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .frac .num,.mw-parser-output .frac .den{font-size:80%;line-height:0;vertical-align:super}.mw-parser-output .frac .den{vertical-align:sub}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>⁄<span class="den">2</span></span>-gerah per day <a href="/wiki/Freight_rate" title="Freight rate">freight rate</a> on a <a href="/wiki/Affreightment" title="Affreightment">contract of affreightment</a> between a charterer and shipmaster, while Law 277 stipulated a <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>⁄<span class="den">6</span></span>-shekel per day freight rate for a 60-gur vessel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sommer_1903_p._88_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sommer_1903_p._88-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harper_1904_p._95_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harper_1904_p._95-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King_1910_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-King_1910-10">[10]</a></sup> In 1816, an archeological excavation in <a href="/wiki/Minya,_Egypt" title="Minya, Egypt">Minya, Egypt</a> (under an <a href="/wiki/Egypt_Eyalet" title="Egypt Eyalet">Eyalet</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>) produced a <a href="/wiki/Nerva%E2%80%93Antonine_dynasty" title="Nerva–Antonine dynasty">Nerva–Antonine dynasty</a>-era <a href="/wiki/Clay_tablet" title="Clay tablet">tablet</a> from the ruins of the <a href="/wiki/Antinous#Deification_and_the_cult_of_Antinous" title="Antinous">Temple of Antinous</a> in <a href="/wiki/Antino%C3%B6polis" title="Antinoöpolis">Antinoöpolis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman_Egypt" title="Roman Egypt">Aegyptus</a> that <a href="/wiki/Articles_of_association" title="Articles of association">prescribed the rules</a> and <a href="/wiki/Benefit_society" title="Benefit society">membership dues</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Burial_society" title="Burial society">burial society</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Collegium_(ancient_Rome)" title="Collegium (ancient Rome)">collegium</a></i> established in <a href="/wiki/Lanuvium" title="Lanuvium">Lanuvium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman_Italy" title="Roman Italy">Italia</a> in approximately 133 AD during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Hadrian" title="Hadrian">Hadrian</a> (117–138) of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>A <i>collegium</i> was any association in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">ancient Rome</a> that <a href="/wiki/Corporation" title="Corporation">acted</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Legal_person" title="Legal person">legal entity</a>. Following the passage of the <i><a href="/wiki/Lex_Julia" title="Lex Julia">Lex Julia</a></i> during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a> as <a href="/wiki/Roman_consul" title="Roman consul">Consul</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_dictator" title="Roman dictator">Dictator</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a> (49–44 BC), and their reaffirmation during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Caesar Augustus</a> as <i><a href="/wiki/Princeps_senatus" title="Princeps senatus">Princeps senatus</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Imperator" title="Imperator">Imperator</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Roman_army" title="Imperial Roman army">Roman Army</a> (27 BC–14 AD), <i>collegia</i> required the approval of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Senate" title="Roman Senate">Roman Senate</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Roman_emperor" title="Roman emperor">Emperor</a> in order to be <a href="/wiki/Charter" title="Charter">authorized as legal bodies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-de_Ligt_2001_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-de_Ligt_2001-14">[14]</a></sup> Ruins at <a href="/wiki/Lambaesis" title="Lambaesis">Lambaesis</a> date the formation of burial societies among Roman Army soldiers and <a href="/wiki/Roman_navy" title="Roman navy">Roman Navy</a> <a href="/wiki/Sailor" title="Sailor">mariners</a> to the reign of <a href="/wiki/Septimius_Severus" title="Septimius Severus">Septimius Severus</a> (193–211) in 198 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> In September 2011, archeological investigations done at the site of the <a href="/wiki/Dredging" title="Dredging">artificial</a> <a href="/wiki/Harbor" title="Harbor">harbor</a> <a href="/wiki/Portus" title="Portus">Portus</a> in <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a> revealed inscriptions in a <a href="/wiki/Shipyard" title="Shipyard">shipyard</a> constructed during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Trajan" title="Trajan">Trajan</a> (98–117) indicating the existence of a shipbuilders guild.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Rome's <a href="/wiki/Ostia_Antica" title="Ostia Antica">La Ostia port</a> was home to a <a href="/wiki/Guildhall" title="Guildhall">guildhall</a> for a <i>corpus naviculariorum</i>, a <i>collegium</i> of <a href="/wiki/Merchant_navy" title="Merchant navy">merchant mariners</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> <i>Collegium</i> also included <a href="/wiki/Fraternity" title="Fraternity">fraternities</a> of <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_ancient_Rome" title="Religion in ancient Rome">Roman priests</a> overseeing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sacrificium_Romanam" title="Sacrificium Romanam">ritual sacrifices</a>, practicing <a href="/wiki/Augury" title="Augury">augury</a>, keeping <a href="/wiki/Religious_text" title="Religious text">scriptures</a>, arranging <a href="/wiki/Roman_festivals" title="Roman festivals">festivals</a>, and maintaining specific <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_cult_of_ancient_Rome" title="Imperial cult of ancient Rome">religious cults</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_trade_unions">Modern trade unions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Modern trade unions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>While a commonly held mistaken view holds modern trade unionism to be a product of <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxism</a>, the earliest modern trade unions predate Marx's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Communist_Manifesto" title="The Communist Manifesto">Communist Manifesto</a></i> (1848) by almost a century, with the first recorded labour strike in the United States by the Philadelphia printers in 1786.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> The origins of modern trade unions can be traced back to 18th-century Britain, where the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">rapid expansion of industrial society</a> then taking place drew masses of people, including women, children, peasants and immigrants into cities. Britain <a href="/wiki/History_of_serfdom" title="History of serfdom">had ended the practice</a> of <a href="/wiki/Serfdom" title="Serfdom">serfdom</a> in 1574, but vast majority of people remained as tenant-farmers on estates owned by landed aristocracy. This transition was not merely one of relocation from rural to urban environs; rather, the nature of industrial work created a new class of "worker". A farmer worked the land, raised animals and grew crop, and either owned the land or paid rent, but ultimately sold a <i>product</i> and had control over his life and work. As industrial workers, however, the workers sold their work as labour and took directions from employers, giving up part of their freedom and self-agency in the service of a master. The critics of the new arrangement would call this "<a href="/wiki/Wage_slavery" title="Wage slavery">wage slavery</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> but the term that persisted was a new form of human relations: <a href="/wiki/Employment" title="Employment">employment</a>. Unlike farmers, workers often had less control over their jobs; without job security or a promise of an on-going relationship with their employers, they lacked some control over the work they performed or how it impacted their health and life. It is in this context, then, that modern trade unions emerge.
</p><p>In the cities, trade unions encountered a large hostility in their early existence from employers and government groups; at the time, unions and unionists were regularly prosecuted under various restraint of trade and conspiracy statutes. This pool of unskilled and semi-skilled labour spontaneously organized in fits and starts throughout its beginnings,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-webb_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-webb-1">[1]</a></sup> and would later be an important arena for the development of trade unions. Trade unions have sometimes been seen as successors to the <a href="/wiki/Guild" title="Guild">guilds</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval_Europe" title="Medieval Europe">medieval Europe</a>, though the relationship between the two is disputed, as the masters of the guilds employed workers (apprentices and journeymen) who were not allowed to organize.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Trade unions and collective bargaining were outlawed from no later than the middle of the 14th century, when the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ordinance_of_Labourers" title="Ordinance of Labourers">Ordinance of Labourers</a> was enacted in the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">Kingdom of England</a>, but their way of thinking was the one that endured down the centuries, inspiring evolutions and advances in thinking which eventually gave workers more power. As collective bargaining and early worker unions grew with the onset of the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>, the government began to clamp down on what it saw as the danger of popular unrest at the time of the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>. In 1799, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Combination_Act" title="Combination Act">Combination Act</a> was passed, which banned trade unions and collective bargaining by British workers. Although the unions were subject to often severe repression until 1824, they were already widespread in cities such as <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>. Workplace militancy had also manifested itself as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luddism" title="Luddism">Luddism</a> and had been prominent in struggles such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1820_Rising" title="1820 Rising">1820 Rising</a> in Scotland, in which 60,000 workers went on a <a href="/wiki/General_strike" title="General strike">general strike</a>, which was soon crushed. Sympathy for the plight of the workers brought repeal of the acts in 1824, although the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Combination_Act_1825" title="Combination Act 1825">Combination Act 1825</a> severely restricted their activity.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>By the 1810s, the first labour organizations to bring together workers of divergent occupations were formed. Possibly the first such union was the General Union of Trades, also known as the Philanthropic Society, founded in 1818 in <a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">Manchester</a>. The latter name was to hide the organization's real purpose in a time when trade unions were still illegal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="National_general_unions">National general unions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: National general unions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:London_Trades_Demonstration.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1272" data-file-width="808" decoding="async" height="268" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/London_Trades_Demonstration.jpg/170px-London_Trades_Demonstration.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/London_Trades_Demonstration.jpg/255px-London_Trades_Demonstration.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/London_Trades_Demonstration.jpg/340px-London_Trades_Demonstration.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:London_Trades_Demonstration.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Poster issued by the London Trades Council, advertising a demonstration held on 2 June 1873</div></div></div>
<p>The first attempts at setting up a national <a href="/wiki/General_union" title="General union">general union</a> were made in the 1820s and 30s. The <a href="/wiki/National_Association_for_the_Protection_of_Labour" title="National Association for the Protection of Labour">National Association for the Protection of Labour</a> was established in 1830 by <a href="/wiki/John_Doherty_(trade_unionist)" title="John Doherty (trade unionist)">John Doherty</a>, after an apparently unsuccessful attempt to create a similar national presence with the National Union of Cotton-spinners. The Association quickly enrolled approximately 150 unions, consisting mostly of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Textile_and_clothing_trade_unions" title="Textile and clothing trade unions">textile related unions</a>, but also including mechanics, blacksmiths, and various others. Membership rose to between 10,000 and 20,000 individuals spread across the five counties of <a href="/wiki/Lancashire" title="Lancashire">Lancashire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cheshire" title="Cheshire">Cheshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Derbyshire" title="Derbyshire">Derbyshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nottinghamshire" title="Nottinghamshire">Nottinghamshire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leicestershire" title="Leicestershire">Leicestershire</a> within a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> To establish awareness and legitimacy, the union started the weekly <i>Voice of the People</i> publication, having the declared intention "to unite the productive classes of the community in one common bond of union."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWebbWebb1894122_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWebbWebb1894122-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1834, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Welsh_People" title="Welsh People">Welsh</a> <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialist</a> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Owen" title="Robert Owen">Robert Owen</a> established the <a href="/wiki/Grand_National_Consolidated_Trades_Union" title="Grand National Consolidated Trades Union">Grand National Consolidated Trades Union</a>. The organization attracted a range of socialists from Owenites to revolutionaries and played a part in the protests after the <a href="/wiki/Tolpuddle_Martyrs" title="Tolpuddle Martyrs">Tolpuddle Martyrs</a>' case, but soon collapsed.
</p><p>More permanent trade unions were established from the 1850s, better resourced but often less radical. The <a href="/wiki/London_Trades_Council" title="London Trades Council">London Trades Council</a> was founded in 1860, and the <a href="/wiki/Sheffield_Outrages" title="Sheffield Outrages">Sheffield Outrages</a> spurred the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Trades_Union_Congress" title="Trades Union Congress">Trades Union Congress</a> in 1868, the first long-lived <a href="/wiki/National_trade_union_center" title="National trade union center">national trade union center</a>. By this time, the existence and the demands of the trade unions were becoming accepted by <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberal</a> <a href="/wiki/Middle_class" title="Middle class">middle class</a> opinion. In <i><a href="/wiki/Principles_of_Political_Economy" title="Principles of Political Economy">Principles of Political Economy</a></i> (1871) <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">John Stuart Mill</a> wrote:
</p>
<blockquote><p>If it were possible for the working classes, by combining among themselves, to raise or keep up the general rate of wages, it needs hardly be said that this would be a thing not to be punished, but to be welcomed and rejoiced at. Unfortunately the effect is quite beyond attainment by such means. The multitudes who compose the working class are too numerous and too widely scattered to combine at all, much more to combine effectually. If they could do so, they might doubtless succeed in diminishing the hours of labour, and obtaining the same wages for less work. They would also have a limited power of obtaining, by combination, an increase of general wages at the expense of profits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p></blockquote><p>Beyond this claim <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">Mill</a> also argued that, because individual workers have no basis for assessing the wages for a particular task, labor unions would lead to greater efficiency of the market system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><h3><span id="Legalization.2C_expansion_and_recognition"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Legalization,_expansion_and_recognition">Legalization, expansion and recognition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Legalization, expansion and recognition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1912_Lawrence_Textile_Strike_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="395" data-file-width="496" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/1912_Lawrence_Textile_Strike_1.jpg/220px-1912_Lawrence_Textile_Strike_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/1912_Lawrence_Textile_Strike_1.jpg/330px-1912_Lawrence_Textile_Strike_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/1912_Lawrence_Textile_Strike_1.jpg/440px-1912_Lawrence_Textile_Strike_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1912_Lawrence_Textile_Strike_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Trade union demonstrators held at bay by soldiers during the 1912 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lawrence_textile_strike" title="Lawrence textile strike">Lawrence textile strike</a> in <a href="/wiki/Lawrence,_Massachusetts" title="Lawrence, Massachusetts">Lawrence, Massachusetts</a></div></div></div>
<p>British trade unions were finally legalized in 1872, after a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_Commission_on_Trade_Unions" title="Royal Commission on Trade Unions">Royal Commission on Trade Unions</a></i> in 1867 agreed that the establishment of the organizations was to the advantage of both employers and employees.
</p><p>This period also saw the growth of trade unions in other industrializing countries, especially the United States, Germany and France.
</p><p>In the United States, the first effective nationwide labour organization was the <a href="/wiki/Knights_of_Labor" title="Knights of Labor">Knights of Labor</a>, in 1869, which began to grow after 1880. Legalization occurred slowly as a result of a series of court decisions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Federation_of_Organized_Trades_and_Labor_Unions" title="Federation of Organized Trades and Labor Unions">Federation of Organized Trades and Labor Unions</a> began in 1881 as a federation of different unions that did not directly enroll workers. In 1886, it became known as the <a href="/wiki/American_Federation_of_Labor" title="American Federation of Labor">American Federation of Labor</a> or AFL.
</p><p>In Germany the <a href="/wiki/Free_Association_of_German_Trade_Unions" title="Free Association of German Trade Unions">Free Association of German Trade Unions</a> was formed in 1897 after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservativism" title="Conservativism">conservative</a> <a href="/wiki/Anti-Socialist_Laws" title="Anti-Socialist Laws">Anti-Socialist Laws</a> of Chancellor <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Bismarck" title="Otto von Bismarck">Otto von Bismarck</a> were repealed.
</p><p>In France, labour organization was illegal until 1884. The Bourse du Travail was founded in 1887 and merged with the Fédération nationale des syndicats (National Federation of Trade Unions) in 1895 to form the <a href="/wiki/General_Confederation_of_Labour_(France)" title="General Confederation of Labour (France)">General Confederation of Labour (France)</a>.
</p><p>In a number of countries during the 20th century, including in Canada, the United States and the United Kingdom, legislation was passed to provide for the voluntary or statutory recognition of a union by an employer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevalence_worldwide">Prevalence worldwide</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Prevalence worldwide">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="OECD">OECD</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: OECD">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Union_density">Union density</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Union density">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The prevalence of labor unions can be measured by "union density", which is expressed as a percentage of the total number of workers in a given location who are trade union members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ILO_Stats_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-ILO_Stats-32">[32]</a></sup> The below table shows the percentage across <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a> members.

</p><p>The union density is especially high for <a href="/wiki/Nordic_countries" title="Nordic countries">Nordic countries</a> with the average being 67% as of 2018.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The union density has been steadily declining from the <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a> average of 35.9% in 1998 to 27.9% in the year 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>The main reasons for these developments are a decline in manufacturing, increased globalization, and governmental policies.
</p><p>The decline in <a href="/wiki/Manufacturing" title="Manufacturing">manufacturing</a> is the most direct one as it generally have been low- or unskilled workers who have benefited the most from labor unions. On the other hand, there might an increase in <a href="/wiki/Developing_country" title="Developing country">developing nations</a> as <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a> nations exported manufacturing industries to these markets. The second reason is <a href="/wiki/Globalization" title="Globalization">globalization</a>, which makes it harder for unions to maintain standards across countries. The last reason is governmental policies. These come from both sides of the political spectrum. In the UK and US, it has been mostly right-wing proposals that make it harder for unions to form or that limit their power. On the other side, there are many policies such as <a href="/wiki/Minimum_wage" title="Minimum wage">minimum wage</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paid_vacation" title="Paid vacation">paid vacation</a>, maternity/paternity leave, etc., that decrease the need to be in a union.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Worldwide">Worldwide</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Worldwide">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Trade_union_density_rate_map.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1398" data-file-width="2754" decoding="async" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Trade_union_density_rate_map.svg/500px-Trade_union_density_rate_map.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Trade_union_density_rate_map.svg/750px-Trade_union_density_rate_map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Trade_union_density_rate_map.svg/1000px-Trade_union_density_rate_map.svg.png 2x" width="500"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Trade_union_density_rate_map.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>World map with countries shaded according to their trade union density rate with statistics provided by the <a href="/wiki/International_Labour_Organization" title="International Labour Organization">International Labour Organization</a> Department of Statistics <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r981673959">.mw-parser-output .legend{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .legend-color{display:inline-block;min-width:1.25em;height:1.25em;line-height:1.25;margin:1px 0;text-align:center;border:1px solid black;background-color:transparent;color:black}.mw-parser-output .legend-text{}</style><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#051d3b; color:white;"> </span> 70.0–79.<span style="text-decoration:overline;">9</span>%</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#0f56b0; </p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Trade_unions_by_country">Trade unions by country</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Trade unions by country">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Australia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Australian_labour_movement" title="Australian labour movement">Australian labour movement</a> generally sought to end <a href="/wiki/Child_labour" title="Child labour">child labour</a> practices, improve <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Worker_safety" title="Worker safety">worker safety</a>, increase wages for both union workers and non-union workers, raise the entire society's <a href="/wiki/Standard_of_living" title="Standard of living">standard of living</a>, reduce the hours in a work week, provide public education for children, and bring other benefits to <a href="/wiki/Working_class" title="Working class">working class</a> families.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Melbourne_Trades_Hall" title="Melbourne Trades Hall">Melbourne Trades Hall</a> was opened in 1859 with <a href="/wiki/Labour_council" title="Labour council">Trades and Labour Councils</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trades_Hall" title="Trades Hall">Trades Halls</a> opening in all cities and most regional towns in the next forty years. During the 1880s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trade_unions" title="Trade unions">Trade unions</a> developed among <a href="/wiki/Sheep_shearer" title="Sheep shearer">shearers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miner" title="Miner">miners</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Stevedore" title="Stevedore">stevedores</a> (wharf workers), but soon spread to cover almost all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue-collar" title="Blue-collar">blue-collar</a> jobs. Shortages of labour led to high wages for a prosperous skilled working class, whose unions demanded and got an <a href="/wiki/Eight-hour_day" title="Eight-hour day">eight-hour day</a> and other benefits unheard of in Europe.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Melbourne_eight_hour_day_march-c1900.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="750" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Melbourne_eight_hour_day_march-c1900.jpg/220px-Melbourne_eight_hour_day_march-c1900.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Melbourne_eight_hour_day_march-c1900.jpg/330px-Melbourne_eight_hour_day_march-c1900.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Melbourne_eight_hour_day_march-c1900.jpg/440px-Melbourne_eight_hour_day_march-c1900.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Melbourne_eight_hour_day_march-c1900.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Eight-hour_day" title="Eight-hour day">Eight-hour day</a> march circa 1900, outside Parliament House in Spring Street, <a href="/wiki/Melbourne" title="Melbourne">Melbourne</a></div></div></div>
<p>Australia gained a reputation as "the working man's paradise". Some employers tried to undercut the unions by importing Chinese labour. This produced a reaction which led to all the colonies restricting Chinese and other Asian immigration. This was the foundation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_Australia_Policy" title="White Australia Policy">White Australia Policy</a>. The "Australian compact", based around centralised industrial arbitration, a degree of government assistance particularly for primary industries, and White Australia, was to continue for many years before gradually dissolving in the second half of the 20th century.
</p><p>In the 1870s and 1880s, the growing <a href="/wiki/Australian_labour_movement" title="Australian labour movement">trade union</a> movement began a series of protests against foreign labour. Their arguments were that Asians and Chinese took jobs away from white men, worked for "substandard" wages, lowered working conditions and refused unionisation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Markey_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Markey-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>Objections to these arguments came largely from wealthy land owners in rural areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Markey_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Markey-36">[36]</a></sup> It was argued that without Asiatics to work in the tropical areas of the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Territory" title="Northern Territory">Northern Territory</a> and Queensland, the area would have to be abandoned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Griffiths_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Griffiths-37">[37]</a></sup> Despite these objections to restricting immigration, between 1875 and 1888 all Australian colonies enacted legislation which excluded all further Chinese immigration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Griffiths_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Griffiths-37">[37]</a></sup> Asian immigrants already residing in the Australian colonies were not expelled and retained the same rights as their Anglo and Southern compatriots.
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barton_Government" title="Barton Government">Barton Government</a> which came to power after the first elections to the Commonwealth parliament in 1901 was formed by the <a href="/wiki/Protectionist_Party" title="Protectionist Party">Protectionist Party</a> with the support of the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Labor_Party" title="Australian Labor Party">Australian Labor Party</a>. The support of the Labor Party was contingent upon restricting non-white immigration, reflecting the attitudes of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Australian_Workers_Union" title="Australian Workers Union">Australian Workers Union</a> and other labour organisations at the time, upon whose support the Labor Party was founded.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Belgium">Belgium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Belgium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_trade_unions_in_Belgium" title="List of trade unions in Belgium">List of trade unions in Belgium</a></div>
<p>With 65% of the workers belonging to a union, <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a> is a country with one of the highest percentages of trade union membership. Only the Scandinavian countries have a higher trade union density. The biggest union with around 1.7 million members is the Christian democrat <a href="/wiki/Confederation_of_Christian_Trade_Unions" title="Confederation of Christian Trade Unions">Confederation of Christian Trade Unions</a> (ACV-CSC) which was founded in 1904.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> The origins of the union can be traced back to the "Anti-Socialist Cotton Workers Union" that was founded in 1886.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> The second biggest union is the socialist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General_Federation_of_Belgian_Labour" title="General Federation of Belgian Labour">General Federation of Belgian Labour</a> (ABVV-FGTB) which has a membership of more than 1.5 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> The ABVV-FGTB traces its origins to 1857, when the first Belgian union was founded in <a href="/wiki/Ghent" title="Ghent">Ghent</a> by a group of <a href="/wiki/Weaving" title="Weaving">weavers</a>. This and other socialist unions became unified around 1898. The ABVV-FGTB in its current form dates back to 1945. The third major multi-sector union in Belgium is the liberal (classical liberal) union <a href="/wiki/General_Confederation_of_Liberal_Trade_Unions_of_Belgium" title="General Confederation of Liberal Trade Unions of Belgium">General Confederation of Liberal Trade Unions of Belgium</a> (ACLVB-CGSLB) which is relatively small in comparison to the first two with a little under 290 thousand members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The ACLVB-CGSLB was founded in 1920 in an effort to unite the many small liberal unions. Back then the liberal union was known as the "Nationale Centrale der Liberale Vakbonden van België". In 1930, the ACLVB-CGSLB adopted its current name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>Besides these "big three" there are a number of smaller unions, some more influential than others. These smaller unions tend to specialize in one profession or economic sector. Next to these specialized unions there is also the <a class="extiw" href="https://nl.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neutrale_en_Onafhankelijke_Vakbond" title="nl:Neutrale en Onafhankelijke Vakbond">Neutral and Independent Union</a> that rejects the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pillarization" title="Pillarization">pillarization</a> of the "big three" trade unions (their affiliation with political parties). There is also a small <a href="/wiki/Flanders" title="Flanders">Flemish</a> nationalist union that exists only in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flemish_language" title="Flemish language">Flemish</a>-speaking part of Belgium, called the <a class="extiw" href="https://nl.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vlaamse_Solidaire_Vakbond" title="nl:Vlaamse Solidaire Vakbond">Vlaamse Solidaire Vakbond</a>. The last Belgian union worth mentioning is the very small, but highly active anarchist union called the <a class="extiw" href="https://nl.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vrije_Bond" title="nl:Vrije Bond">Vrije Bond</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Canada">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_trade_unions_in_Canada" title="List of trade unions in Canada">List of trade unions in Canada</a></div>
<p>Canada's first trade union, the Labourers' Benevolent Association (now International Longshoremen's Association Local 273), formed in <a href="/wiki/Saint_John,_New_Brunswick" title="Saint John, New Brunswick">Saint John, New Brunswick</a> in 1849. The union was formed when Saint John's longshoremen banded together to lobby for regular pay and a shorter workday.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Canadian unionism had early ties with <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">Britain</a> and Ireland. Tradesmen who came from Britain brought traditions of the British trade union movement, and many British unions had branches in Canada. Canadian unionism's ties with the United States eventually replaced those with Britain.
</p><p>Collective bargaining was first recognized in 1945, after the strike by the <a href="/wiki/United_Auto_Workers" title="United Auto Workers">United Auto Workers</a> at the <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors</a>' plant in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oshawa,_Ontario" title="Oshawa, Ontario">Oshawa, Ontario</a>.
Justice <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Rand" title="Ivan Rand">Ivan Rand</a> issued a landmark legal decision after the strike in <a href="/wiki/Windsor,_Ontario" title="Windsor, Ontario">Windsor, Ontario</a>, involving 17,000 <a href="/wiki/Ford_Motor_Company" title="Ford Motor Company">Ford</a> workers. He granted the union the compulsory check-off of union dues. Rand ruled that all workers in a bargaining unit benefit from a union-negotiated contract. Therefore, he reasoned they must pay union dues, although they do not have to join the union.
</p><p>The post-<a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> era also saw an increased pattern of unionization in the public service. Teachers, nurses, social workers, professors and cultural workers (those employed in museums, orchestras and art galleries) all sought private-sector collective bargaining rights. The <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Labour_Congress" title="Canadian Labour Congress">Canadian Labour Congress</a> was founded in 1956 as the <a href="/wiki/National_trade_union_center" title="National trade union center">national trade union center</a> for Canada.
</p><p>In the 1970s the federal government came under intense pressures to curtail labour cost and inflation. In 1975, the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_of_Canada" title="Liberal Party of Canada">Liberal</a> government of <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Trudeau" title="Pierre Trudeau">Pierre Trudeau</a> introduced mandatory price and wage controls. Under the new law, wages increases were monitored and those ruled to be unacceptably high were rolled back by the government.
</p><p>Pressures on unions continued into the 1980s and '90s. Private sector unions faced plant closures in many manufacturing industries and demands to reduce wages and increase productivity. Public sector unions came under attack by federal and provincial governments as they attempted to reduce spending, reduce taxes and balance budgets. Legislation was introduced in many jurisdictions reversing union collective bargaining rights, and many jobs were lost to contractors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Prominent domestic unions in Canada include <a href="/wiki/ACTRA" title="ACTRA">ACTRA</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Union_of_Postal_Workers" title="Canadian Union of Postal Workers">Canadian Union of Postal Workers</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Union_of_Public_Employees" title="Canadian Union of Public Employees">Canadian Union of Public Employees</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Public_Service_Alliance_of_Canada" title="Public Service Alliance of Canada">Public Service Alliance of Canada</a>, the <a href="/wiki/National_Union_of_Public_and_General_Employees" title="National Union of Public and General Employees">National Union of Public and General Employees</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Unifor" title="Unifor">Unifor</a>. International unions active in Canada include the <a href="/wiki/International_Alliance_of_Theatrical_Stage_Employees" title="International Alliance of Theatrical Stage Employees">International Alliance of Theatrical Stage Employees</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Automobile_Workers" title="United Automobile Workers">United Automobile Workers</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_Food_and_Commercial_Workers" title="United Food and Commercial Workers">United Food and Commercial Workers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/United_Steelworkers" title="United Steelworkers">United Steelworkers</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colombia">Colombia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Colombia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Trade_unions_in_Colombia" title="Trade unions in Colombia">Trade unions in Colombia</a></div>
<p>Until around 1990 Colombian trade unions were among the strongest in <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JFA11_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-JFA11-45">[45]</a></sup> However, the 1980s expansion of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paramilitarism_in_Colombia" title="Paramilitarism in Colombia">paramilitarism in Colombia</a> saw trade union leaders and members increasingly targeted for assassination, and as a result Colombia has been the most dangerous country in the world for trade unionists for several decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Between 2000 and 2010 Colombia accounted for 63.1% of trade unionists murdered globally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ITUCres_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-ITUCres-49">[49]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/International_Trade_Union_Confederation" title="International Trade Union Confederation">International Trade Union Confederation</a> (ITUC) there were 2832 murders of trade unionists between 1 January 1986 and 30 April 2010,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ITUCres_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-ITUCres-49">[49]</a></sup> meaning that "on average, men and women trade unionists in Colombia have been killed at the rate of one every three days over the last 23 years."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ITUC2010_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-ITUC2010-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Costa_Rica">Costa Rica</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Costa Rica">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Trade_unions_in_Costa_Rica" title="Trade unions in Costa Rica">Trade unions in Costa Rica</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Agricultores,_manifestaci%C3%B3n_San_Jos%C3%A9_Costa_Rica,_enero_2011.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Agricultores%2C_manifestaci%C3%B3n_San_Jos%C3%A9_Costa_Rica%2C_enero_2011.jpg/220px-Agricultores%2C_manifestaci%C3%B3n_San_Jos%C3%A9_Costa_Rica%2C_enero_2011.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Agricultores%2C_manifestaci%C3%B3n_San_Jos%C3%A9_Costa_Rica%2C_enero_2011.jpg/330px-Agricultores%2C_manifestaci%C3%B3n_San_Jos%C3%A9_Costa_Rica%2C_enero_2011.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Agricultores%2C_manifestaci%C3%B3n_San_Jos%C3%A9_Costa_Rica%2C_enero_2011.jpg/440px-Agricultores%2C_manifestaci%C3%B3n_San_Jos%C3%A9_Costa_Rica%2C_enero_2011.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Agricultores,_manifestaci%C3%B3n_San_Jos%C3%A9_Costa_Rica,_enero_2011.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Costa Rican agricultural unions demonstration, January 2011</div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica" title="Costa Rica">Costa Rica</a>, trade unions first appeared in the late 1800s to support workers in a variety of urban and industrial jobs, such as railroad builders and craft tradesmen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SITRAPEQUIA_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-SITRAPEQUIA-51">[51]</a></sup> After facing violent repression, such as during the 1934 United Fruit Strike, unions gained more power after the 1948 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Costa_Rican_Civil_War" title="Costa Rican Civil War">Costa Rican Civil War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SITRAPEQUIA_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-SITRAPEQUIA-51">[51]</a></sup> Today, Costa Rican unions are strongest in the public sector, including the fields of education and medicine, but also have a strong presence in the agricultural sector.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SITRAPEQUIA_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-SITRAPEQUIA-51">[51]</a></sup> In general, Costa Rican unions support government regulation of the banking, medical, and education fields, as well as improved wages and working conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Germany">Germany</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Germany">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Trade_unions_in_Germany" title="Trade unions in Germany">Trade unions in Germany</a></div>
<p>Trade unions in Germany have a history reaching back to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Revolutions_of_1848_in_the_German_states" title="Revolutions of 1848 in the German states">German revolution</a> in 1848, and still play an important role in the <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Germany" title="Economy of Germany">German economy</a> and society. In 1875 the SPD, the Social Democratic Party of Germany, which is one of the biggest political parties in Germany, at first supported the forming of unions in Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> However, according to John A. Moses, the German trade unions were not directly affiliated with the Social Democratic Party. The SPD leadership insisted on the primacy of politics, and refused to emphasize support for union goals and methods. The unions led <a href="/wiki/Carl_Legien" title="Carl Legien">Carl Legien</a> (1861-1920) developed their own nonpartisan political goals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the early 1930s, according to Gerard Braunthal, the three main trade unions (Allgemeiner Deutscher Gewerkschaftsbund, Allgemeiner freier Angestelltenbund, and Allgemeiner Deutscher Beamtenbund) failed to actively oppose Hitler in 1932–33. They minimized the threat in 1932 and opposed a general strike because it might spark a civil war. As the Nazis took power in 1933, the high unemployment had demoralized workers. Their historic faith in socialism gave way to a wave of nationalism. The leaders did not foresee how the Nazis would completely unseat them and suppress labor's aspirations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>The most important labour organisation is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_Confederation_of_Trade_Unions" title="German Confederation of Trade Unions">German Confederation of Trade Unions</a> <i>(Deutscher Gewerkschaftsbund – DGB)</i>, which represents more than 6 million workers in 2011. It is the umbrella association of several single trade unions for special economic sectors. The DGB is not the only Union Organization that represents the working trade. There are smaller organizations, such as the CGB, which is a Christian-based confederation, that represent over 1.5 million workers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Trade_unions_in_India" title="Trade unions in India">Trade unions in India</a></div>
<p>In India, the Trade Union movement is generally divided on political lines. According to provisional statistics from the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Labour_and_Employment_(India)" title="Ministry of Labour and Employment (India)">Ministry of Labour</a>, trade unions had a combined membership of 24,601,589 in 2002. As of 2008, there are 12 Central Trade Union Organisations (CTUO) recognized by the Ministry of Labour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> The forming of these unions was a big deal in India. It led to a big push for more regulatory laws which gave workers a lot more power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AITUC" title="AITUC">AITUC</a> is the oldest trade union in India. It is a left supported organization.
A trade union with nearly 2,000,000 members is the Self Employed Women's Association (SEWA) which protects the rights of Indian women working in the informal economy. In addition to the protection of rights, SEWA educates, mobilizes, finances, and exalts their members' trades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Multiple other organizations represent workers. These organizations are formed upon different political groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> These different groups allow different groups of people with different political views to join a Union.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Japan">Japan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Japan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Labor_unions_in_Japan" title="Labor unions in Japan">Labor unions in Japan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NUGW_May_Day_2011.jpg"><img alt="NUGW May Day 2011" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="266" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/NUGW_May_Day_2011.jpg/220px-NUGW_May_Day_2011.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/NUGW_May_Day_2011.jpg/330px-NUGW_May_Day_2011.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/35/NUGW_May_Day_2011.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NUGW_May_Day_2011.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>2011 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zenrokyo" title="Zenrokyo">National Trade Union Council (<i>Zenrokyo</i>)</a> <a href="/wiki/International_Workers%27_Day#Japan" title="International Workers' Day">May Day</a> march, Tokyo</div></div></div>
<p>Trade unions emerged in Japan in the second half of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meiji_period" title="Meiji period">Meiji period</a> as the country underwent a period of rapid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industrialization" title="Industrialization">industrialization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nimura_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nimura-62">[62]</a></sup> Until 1945, however, the labour movement remained weak, impeded by lack of legal rights,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cross_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cross-63">[63]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-union" title="Anti-union">anti-union</a> legislation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nimura_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nimura-62">[62]</a></sup> management-organised factory councils, and political divisions between "cooperative" and radical unionists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weathers_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weathers-64">[64]</a></sup> In the immediate aftermath of the Second World War, the <a href="/wiki/Occupation_of_Japan" title="Occupation of Japan">US Occupation</a> authorities initially encouraged the formation of independent unions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cross_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cross-63">[63]</a></sup> Legislation was passed that enshrined the right to organise,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> and membership rapidly rose to 5 million by February 1947.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cross_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cross-63">[63]</a></sup> The organisation rate, however, peaked at 55.8% in 1949 and subsequently declined to 18.2% (2006).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jil_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jil-66">[66]</a></sup> The labour movement went through a process of reorganisation from 1987 to 1991<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> from which emerged the present configuration of three major trade union federations, <a href="/wiki/RENGO" title="RENGO">Rengo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zenroren" title="Zenroren">Zenroren</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zenrokyo" title="Zenrokyo">Zenrokyo</a>, along with other smaller national union organisations.
</p>
<h3><span id="Lithuania.2C_Latvia_and_Estonia"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Lithuania,_Latvia_and_Estonia">Lithuania, Latvia and Estonia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Lithuania, Latvia and Estonia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the three Baltic countries the independent trade unions was an aspect of almost every worker's life during the period of <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet</a> rule from 1944 to 1991. The trade union system was closely integrated with that of the <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Communist Party of the Soviet Union">Communist Party of the Soviet Union</a>. After the regaining of national independence in 1990–1991 the trade unions in Lithuania, Latvia and Estonia have experienced rapid loss of membership and <a href="/wiki/Economic_power" title="Economic power">economic power</a>, while employers' organisations have increased both in power and membership. Low financial and organisational capacity caused by declining membership adds to the problem of interest definition, aggregation and <a href="/wiki/Protection" title="Protection">protection</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Negotiations" title="Negotiations">negotiations</a> with employers' and state organisations. Even the difference exists in the way of organization trade union and density. Starting from 2008 the union density slightly decrease in <a href="/wiki/Latvia" title="Latvia">Latvia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a>. In case of <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a> this indicator is lower than in <a href="/wiki/Latvia" title="Latvia">Latvia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a> but stays stable average 7 percent from total number of <a href="/wiki/Employment" title="Employment">employment</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Historical legitimacy is one of the negative factors that determine low associational power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pjms.zim.pcz.pl_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-pjms.zim.pcz.pl-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mexico">Mexico</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Mexico">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Mexican_labor_law" title="Mexican labor law">Mexican labor law</a></div>
<p>Before the 1990s, unions in Mexico had been historically part of a state institutional system. From 1940 until the 1980s, during the worldwide spread of <a href="/wiki/Neoliberalism" title="Neoliberalism">neoliberalism</a> through the <a href="/wiki/Washington_Consensus" title="Washington Consensus">Washington Consensus</a>, the Mexican unions did not operate independently, but instead as part of a state institutional system, largely controlled by the ruling party.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BotzJay01052010_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-BotzJay01052010-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>During these 40 years, the primary aim of the trade unions was not to benefit the workers, but to carry out the state's economic policy under their cosy relationship with the ruling party. This economic policy, which peaked in the 1950s and 60s with the so-called "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mexican_Miracle" title="Mexican Miracle">Mexican Miracle</a>", saw rising incomes and improved standards of living but the primary beneficiaries were the wealthy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BotzJay01052010_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-BotzJay01052010-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1980s, Mexico began adhering to Washington Consensus policies, selling off state industries such as railroad and telecommunications to private industries. The new owners had an antagonistic attitude towards unions, which, accustomed to comfortable relationships with the state, were not prepared to fight back. A movement of <a href="/wiki/New_unionism" title="New unionism">new unions</a> began to emerge under a more independent model, while the former institutionalized unions had become very corrupt, violent, and led by gangsters. From the 1990s onwards, this new model of independent unions prevailed, a number of them represented by the National Union of Workers / Unión Nacional de Trabajadores.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BotzJay01052010_70-2"><a href="#cite_note-BotzJay01052010-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>Current old institutions like the Oil Workers Union and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Education_Workers%27_Union" title="National Education Workers' Union">National Education Workers' Union</a> (<i>Sindicato Nacional de Trabajadores de la Educación</i>, or SNTE) are examples of how the use of government benefits are not being applied to improve the quality in the investigation of the use of oil or the basic education in Mexico as long as their leaders show publicly that they are living wealthily. With 1.4 million members, the teachers' union is <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a>'s largest; half of Mexico's government employees are teachers. It controls school curriculums, and all teacher appointments. Until recently, retiring teachers routinely "gave" their lifelong appointment to a relative or "sell" it for anywhere in between $4,700 and $11,800.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wsj_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-wsj-72">[72]</a></sup>
