</p><p>In 2022, Sindicato independiente nacional de trabajadores trabajadoras de la industria automotriz, SINTTIA, a union backed by American and Canadian unions won a union representation election at a <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors</a> plant in the city of <a href="/wiki/Silao" title="Silao">Silao</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Confederation_of_Mexican_Workers" title="Confederation of Mexican Workers">Confederation of Mexican Workers</a> (CTM), a union affiliated with the <a href="/wiki/Institutional_Revolutionary_Party" title="Institutional Revolutionary Party">Institutional Revolutionary Party</a> (PRI) which had negotiated <a href="/wiki/Sweetheart_deal" title="Sweetheart deal">sweet-heart contracts</a> with GM since the opening of the plant in 1995, and an allied "independent" union received only small percentages of the vote. A worker at the plant with 10 years service reported wages of 480 pesos ($23.27) for a 12-hour shift. At <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen" title="Volkswagen">Volkswagen</a>'s plant in <a href="/wiki/Puebla" title="Puebla">Puebla</a> state, the union has negotiated average pay of 600 pesos ($29.15) a day for an eight-hour shift.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reuters2322_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters2322-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nordic_countries">Nordic countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Nordic countries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Striking_workers_organised_in_the_Norwegian_labour_union_UNIO.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Striking_workers_organised_in_the_Norwegian_labour_union_UNIO.JPG/220px-Striking_workers_organised_in_the_Norwegian_labour_union_UNIO.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Striking_workers_organised_in_the_Norwegian_labour_union_UNIO.JPG/330px-Striking_workers_organised_in_the_Norwegian_labour_union_UNIO.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Striking_workers_organised_in_the_Norwegian_labour_union_UNIO.JPG/440px-Striking_workers_organised_in_the_Norwegian_labour_union_UNIO.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Striking_workers_organised_in_the_Norwegian_labour_union_UNIO.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Workers on strike in Oslo, Norway, 2012</div></div></div>
<p>Trade unions (Danish: <i>Fagforeninger</i>, Norwegian: <i>Fagforeninger/Fagforeiningar</i>, Swedish: <i>Fackföreningar</i>, Finnish: <i>Ammattiliitot</i>) have a long tradition in <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nordic_countries" title="Nordic countries">Nordic</a> society. Beginning in the mid-19th century, they today have a large impact on the nature of employment and workers' rights in many of the <a href="/wiki/Nordic_countries" title="Nordic countries">Nordic countries</a>. One of the largest trade unions in <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swedish_Confederation_of_Trade_Unions" title="Swedish Confederation of Trade Unions">Swedish Confederation of Trade Unions</a>, (LO, <i>Landsorganisationen</i>), incorporating unions such as the Swedish Metal Workers' Union (<i>IF Metall</i> = <i>Industrifacket Metall</i>), the Swedish Electricians' Union (Svenska Elektrikerförbundet) and the Swedish Municipality Workers' Union (<i>Svenska Kommunalarbetareförbundet</i>, abbreviated <i>Kommunal</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> One of the aims of IF Metall is to transform jobs into "good jobs", also called "developing jobs".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Swedish system is strongly based on the so-called Swedish model, which argues the importance of collective agreements between trade unions and employers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pjms.zim.pcz.pl_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-pjms.zim.pcz.pl-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>Today, the world's highest rates of union membership are in the <a href="/wiki/Nordic_countries" title="Nordic countries">Nordic countries</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> As of 2018 or latest year, the percentage of workers belonging to a union (trade union density) was 90.4% in <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a>, 67.2% in <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>, 66.1% in <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, 64.4% in <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> and 52.5% in <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, while it is unknown in <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faroe Islands</a> and <a href="/wiki/%C3%85land" title="Åland">Åland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Excluding full-time students working part-time, Swedish union density was 68% in 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> In all the Nordic countries with a <a href="/wiki/Ghent_system" title="Ghent system">Ghent system</a>—Sweden,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Denmark and <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>—union density is about 70%. The considerably raised membership fees of Swedish union unemployment funds implemented by the new center-right government in January 2007 caused large drops in membership in both unemployment funds and trade unions. From 2006 to 2008, union density declined by six percentage points: from 77% to 71%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spain">Spain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Spain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_trade_unions_in_Spain" title="List of trade unions in Spain">List of trade unions in Spain</a></div>
<p>During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_civil_war" title="Spanish civil war">Spanish civil war</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anarchists" title="Anarchists">anarchists</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syndicalists" title="Syndicalists">syndicalists</a> took control over much of Spain. Implementing worker control through a system of <a href="/wiki/Libertarian_socialism" title="Libertarian socialism">libertarian socialism</a> with organizations like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anarcho-syndicalist" title="Anarcho-syndicalist">anarcho-syndicalist</a> <a href="/wiki/Confederaci%C3%B3n_Nacional_del_Trabajo" title="Confederación Nacional del Trabajo">CNT</a> organizing throughout Spain. Unions were particularly present in <a href="/wiki/Revolutionary_Catalonia" title="Revolutionary Catalonia">Revolutionary Catalonia</a>, in which anarchists were already the basis for most of society with over 90% of industries being organized through work cooperatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> The republicans, anarchists and leftists would later lose control over Spain, with <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Franco" title="Francisco Franco">Francisco Franco</a> becoming dictator of Spain.
</p><p>During the fascist regime of Spain the Francoist regime saw the worker movement and union movement as a threat, Franco banned all existing trade unions and set up the government controlled <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Syndical_Organization" title="Spanish Syndical Organization">Spanish Syndical Organization</a> as the only legal Spanish trade union, with the organization existing to maintain Franco's power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many anarchists, communists and leftists turned towards insurgent tactics as Franco implemented wide reaching authoritarian policies, with the CNT and other unions being forced underground. Anarchists would operate covertly setting up local organizations and underground movements to challenge Franco.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> On the 20 of December the <a href="/wiki/ETA_(separatist_group)" title="ETA (separatist group)">ETA</a> <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Luis_Carrero_Blanco" title="Assassination of Luis Carrero Blanco">assassinated Luis Carrero</a>. The death of Carrero Blanco had numerous political implications. By the end of 1973, the physical health of Francisco Franco had declined significantly, and it epitomized the final crisis of the Francoist regime. After his death, the most conservative sector of the Francoist State, known as the búnker, wanted to influence Franco so that he would choose an ultraconservative as Prime Minister. Finally, he chose Carlos Arias Navarro, who originally announced a partial relaxation of the most rigid aspects of the Francoist State, but quickly retreated under pressure from the búnker. After Franco's death Arias Navarro began relaxing Spanish authoritarianism.
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_transition_to_democracy" title="Spanish transition to democracy">Spanish transition to democracy</a>, leftist organizations became legal once again. In modern Spain trade unions now contribute massively towards Spanish society, being again the main catalyst for political change in Spain, with cooperatives employing large parts of the Spanish population such as the <a href="/wiki/Mondragon_Corporation" title="Mondragon Corporation">Mondragon Corporation</a>. Trade unions today lead mass protests against the Spanish government, and are one of the main vectors of political change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom">United Kingdom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: United Kingdom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Trade_unions_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Trade unions in the United Kingdom">Trade unions in the United Kingdom</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_trade_unions_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="History of trade unions in the United Kingdom">History of trade unions in the United Kingdom</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leeds_public_sector_pensions_strike_in_November_2011_9.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1067" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Leeds_public_sector_pensions_strike_in_November_2011_9.jpg/220px-Leeds_public_sector_pensions_strike_in_November_2011_9.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Leeds_public_sector_pensions_strike_in_November_2011_9.jpg/330px-Leeds_public_sector_pensions_strike_in_November_2011_9.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Leeds_public_sector_pensions_strike_in_November_2011_9.jpg/440px-Leeds_public_sector_pensions_strike_in_November_2011_9.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leeds_public_sector_pensions_strike_in_November_2011_9.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Public sector workers in <a href="/wiki/Leeds" title="Leeds">Leeds</a> striking over pension changes by the government in November 2011</div></div></div>
<p>Moderate <a href="/wiki/New_Model_Union" title="New Model Union">New Model Unions</a> dominated the union movement from the mid-19th century and where trade unionism was stronger than the political labour movement until the formation and growth of the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a> in the early years of the 20th century.
</p><p>Trade unionism in the United Kingdom was a major factor in some of the economic crises during the 1960s and the 1970s, culminating in the "<a href="/wiki/Winter_of_Discontent" title="Winter of Discontent">Winter of Discontent</a>" of late-1978 and early-1979, when a significant percentage of the nation's public sector workers went on strike. By this stage, some 12,000,000 workers in the United Kingdom were trade union members. However, the election victory of the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative Party</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a> at the <a href="/wiki/1979_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1979 United Kingdom general election">1979 general election</a>, at the expense of Labour's <a href="/wiki/James_Callaghan" title="James Callaghan">James Callaghan</a>, saw substantial trade union reform which saw the level of strikes fall. 
The level of trade union membership also fell sharply in the 1980s, and continued falling for most of the 1990s. The long decline of most of the industries in which manual trade unions were strong—e.g. steel, coal, printing, the docks—was one of the causes of this loss of trade union members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2011, there were 6,135,126 members in TUC-affiliated unions, down from a peak of 12,172,508 in 1980. Trade union density was 14.1% in the private sector and 56.5% in the public sector.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Labor_unions_in_the_United_States" title="Labor unions in the United States">Labor unions in the United States</a> and <a href="/wiki/Labor_history_of_the_United_States" title="Labor history of the United States">Labor history of the United States</a></div>
<p>Labor unions are legally recognized as representatives of workers in many industries in the United States. In the United States, unions were formed based on power with the people, not over the people like the government at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Their activity today centres on <a href="/wiki/Collective_bargaining" title="Collective bargaining">collective bargaining</a> over wages, benefits and working conditions for their membership, and on representing their members in disputes with management over violations of contract provisions. Larger unions also typically engage in lobbying activities and supporting endorsed candidates at the state and federal level.
</p><p>Most unions in America are aligned with one of two larger umbrella organizations: the <a href="/wiki/AFL%E2%80%93CIO" title="AFL–CIO">AFL–CIO</a> created in 1955, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Change_to_Win_Federation" title="Change to Win Federation">Change to Win Federation</a> which split from the AFL-CIO in 2005. Both advocate policies and legislation on behalf of workers in the United States and Canada, and take an active role in politics. The AFL–CIO is especially concerned with global trade issues.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Midnight_at_the_glassworks2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3256" data-file-width="4803" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Midnight_at_the_glassworks2.jpg/220px-Midnight_at_the_glassworks2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Midnight_at_the_glassworks2.jpg/330px-Midnight_at_the_glassworks2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Midnight_at_the_glassworks2.jpg/440px-Midnight_at_the_glassworks2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Midnight_at_the_glassworks2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Child_labour" title="Child labour">Child labourers</a> in an <a href="/wiki/Indiana" title="Indiana">Indiana</a> glass works. Labor unions have an objective interest in combating child labour.</div></div></div>
<p>In 2010, the percentage of workers belonging to a union in the United States (or total labor union "density") was 11.4%, compared to 18.3% in Japan, 27.5% in Canada and 70% in Finland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>The most prominent unions are among <a href="/wiki/Public_sector" title="Public sector">public sector</a> employees such as teachers, police and other non-managerial or non-executive federal, state, county and municipal employees. Members of unions are disproportionately older, male and residents of the Northeast, the Midwest, and California.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yeselson_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yeselson-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>The majority of union members come from the public sector. Nearly 34.8% of public sector employees are union members. In the private sector, just 6.3% of employees are union members<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BLS01272012_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-BLS01272012-90">[90]</a></sup>—levels not seen since 1932<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>.
</p><p>Union workers in the private sector average 10–30% higher pay than non-union in America after controlling for individual, job, and labour market characteristics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Because of their inherently governmental function, public sector workers are paid the same regardless of union affiliation or non-affiliation after controlling for individual, job, and labour market characteristics.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Vatican_.28Holy_See.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Vatican_(Holy_See)">Vatican (Holy See)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Vatican (Holy See)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Association_of_Vatican_Lay_Workers" title="Association of Vatican Lay Workers">Association of Vatican Lay Workers</a> represents lay employees in the Vatican.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure_and_politics">Structure and politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Structure and politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cesar_chavez2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1827" data-file-width="2778" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Cesar_chavez2.jpg/220px-Cesar_chavez2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Cesar_chavez2.jpg/330px-Cesar_chavez2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Cesar_chavez2.jpg/440px-Cesar_chavez2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cesar_chavez2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cesar_Chavez" title="Cesar Chavez">Cesar Chavez</a> speaking at a 1974 <a href="/wiki/United_Farm_Workers" title="United Farm Workers">United Farm Workers</a> rally in <a href="/wiki/Delano,_California" title="Delano, California">Delano, California</a>. The UFW during Chavez's tenure was committed to restricting immigration.</div></div></div>
<p>Unions may organize a particular section of skilled workers (<a href="/wiki/Craft_unionism" title="Craft unionism">craft unionism</a>, traditionally found in Australia, Canada, Denmark, Norway, Sweden, Switzerland, the UK and the US<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poole,_M._1986_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Poole,_M._1986-2">[2]</a></sup>), a cross-section of workers from various trades (<a href="/wiki/General_union" title="General union">general unionism</a>, traditionally found in Australia, Belgium, Canada, Denmark, Netherlands, the UK and the US), or attempt to organize all workers within a particular industry (<a href="/wiki/Industrial_unionism" title="Industrial unionism">industrial unionism</a>, found in Australia, Canada, Germany, Finland, Norway, South Korea, Sweden, Switzerland, the UK and the US). These unions are often divided into "<a href="/wiki/Local_union" title="Local union">locals</a>", and united in national <a href="/wiki/List_of_federations_of_trade_unions" title="List of federations of trade unions">federations</a>. These federations themselves will affiliate with <a href="/wiki/International_organization" title="International organization">Internationals</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/International_Trade_Union_Confederation" title="International Trade Union Confederation">International Trade Union Confederation</a>. However, in Japan, union organization is slightly different due to the presence of enterprise unions, i.e. unions that are specific to a plant or company. These enterprise unions, however, join industry-wide federations which in turn are members of <a href="/wiki/RENGO" title="RENGO">Rengo</a>, the Japanese national trade union confederation.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Professional_association" title="Professional association">professional associations</a> often carry out the functions of a trade union. In these cases, they may be negotiating for white-collar or professional workers, such as physicians, engineers or teachers. Typically such trade unions refrain from politics or pursue a more liberal politics than their blue-collar counterparts.
</p><p>A union may acquire the status of a "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Juristic_person" title="Juristic person">juristic person</a>" (an artificial legal entity), with a mandate to negotiate with employers for the workers it represents. In such cases, unions have certain legal rights, most importantly the right to engage in <a href="/wiki/Collective_bargaining" title="Collective bargaining">collective bargaining</a> with the employer (or employers) over wages, working hours, and other <a href="/wiki/Employment_contract" title="Employment contract">terms and conditions of employment</a>. The inability of the parties to reach an agreement may lead to <a href="/wiki/Industrial_action" title="Industrial action">industrial action</a>, culminating in either <a href="/wiki/Strike_action" title="Strike action">strike action</a> or management <a href="/wiki/Lockout_(industry)" title="Lockout (industry)">lockout</a>, or binding arbitration. In extreme cases, violent or illegal activities may develop around these events.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Great_Railway_Strike_1886_-_E_St_Louis.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="930" data-file-width="1449" decoding="async" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Great_Railway_Strike_1886_-_E_St_Louis.jpg/220px-Great_Railway_Strike_1886_-_E_St_Louis.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Great_Railway_Strike_1886_-_E_St_Louis.jpg/330px-Great_Railway_Strike_1886_-_E_St_Louis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Great_Railway_Strike_1886_-_E_St_Louis.jpg/440px-Great_Railway_Strike_1886_-_E_St_Louis.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Great_Railway_Strike_1886_-_E_St_Louis.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Southwest_Railroad_Strike_of_1886" title="Great Southwest Railroad Strike of 1886">Great Southwest Railroad Strike of 1886</a> was a trade union strike involving more than 200,000 workers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In other circumstances, unions may not have the legal right to represent workers, or the right may be in question. This lack of status can range from non-recognition of a union to political or criminal prosecution of union activists and members, with many cases of violence and deaths having been recorded historically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>Unions may also engage in broader political or social struggle. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_Movement_Unionism" title="Social Movement Unionism">Social Unionism</a> encompasses many unions that use their organizational strength to advocate for social policies and legislation favourable to their members or to workers in general. As well, unions in some countries are closely aligned with <a href="/wiki/Political_party" title="Political party">political parties</a>.
</p><p>Unions are also delineated by the <a href="/wiki/Service_model" title="Service model">service model</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Organizing_model" title="Organizing model">organizing model</a>. The service model union focuses more on maintaining worker rights, providing services, and resolving disputes. Alternately, the organizing model typically involves full-time <a href="/wiki/Union_organizer" title="Union organizer">union organizers</a>, who work by building up confidence, strong networks, and leaders within the workforce; and confrontational campaigns involving large numbers of union members. Many unions are a blend of these two philosophies, and the definitions of the models themselves are still debated.
</p><p>In Britain, the perceived left-leaning nature of trade unions has resulted in the formation of a reactionary right-wing trade union called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solidarity_%E2%80%93_The_Union_for_British_Workers" title="Solidarity – The Union for British Workers">Solidarity</a> which is supported by the far-right <a href="/wiki/British_National_Party" title="British National Party">BNP</a>. In Denmark, there are some newer apolitical "discount" unions who offer a very basic level of services, as opposed to the dominating Danish pattern of extensive services and organizing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Unison_strike_rally_Oxford_20060328.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1168" data-file-width="1760" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Unison_strike_rally_Oxford_20060328.jpg/220px-Unison_strike_rally_Oxford_20060328.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Unison_strike_rally_Oxford_20060328.jpg/330px-Unison_strike_rally_Oxford_20060328.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Unison_strike_rally_Oxford_20060328.jpg/440px-Unison_strike_rally_Oxford_20060328.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Unison_strike_rally_Oxford_20060328.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A rally of the trade union <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UNISON" title="UNISON">UNISON</a> in <a href="/wiki/Oxford" title="Oxford">Oxford</a> during a strike on 28 March 2006</div></div></div>
<p>In contrast, in several European countries (e.g. Belgium, Denmark, the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>), religious unions have existed for decades. These unions typically distanced themselves from some of the doctrines of orthodox <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxism</a>, such as the preference of atheism and from rhetoric suggesting that employees' interests always are in conflict with those of employers. Some of these Christian unions have had some ties to centrist or conservative political movements and some do not regard strikes as acceptable political means for achieving employees' goals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poole,_M._1986_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Poole,_M._1986-2">[2]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, the biggest trade union <a href="/wiki/Solidarity_(Polish_trade_union)" title="Solidarity (Polish trade union)">Solidarity</a> emerged as an <a href="/wiki/Anti-communism" title="Anti-communism">anti-communist</a> movement with <a href="/wiki/Religious_nationalism" title="Religious nationalism">religious nationalist</a> overtones<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> and today it supports the right-wing <a href="/wiki/Law_and_Justice" title="Law and Justice">Law and Justice</a> party.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although their political structure and autonomy varies widely, union leaderships are usually formed through democratic <a href="/wiki/Election" title="Election">elections</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> Some research, such as that conducted by the Australian Centre for Industrial Relations Research and Training,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> argues that unionized workers enjoy better conditions and wages than those who are not unionized.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Shop_types">Shop types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Shop types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Companies that employ workers with a union generally operate on one of several models:
</p>
<ul><li>A <a href="/wiki/Closed_shop" title="Closed shop">closed shop</a> (US) or a "pre-entry closed shop" (UK) employs only people who are already union members. The compulsory <a href="/wiki/Hiring_hall" title="Hiring hall">hiring hall</a> is an example of a closed shop—in this case the employer must recruit directly from the union, as well as the employee working strictly for unionized employers.</li>
<li>A <a href="/wiki/Union_shop" title="Union shop">union shop</a> (US) or a "post-entry closed shop" (UK) employs non-union workers as well, but sets a time limit within which new employees must join a union.</li>
<li>An <a href="/wiki/Agency_shop" title="Agency shop">agency shop</a> requires non-union workers to pay a fee to the union for its services in negotiating their contract. This is sometimes called the <a href="/wiki/Rand_formula" title="Rand formula">Rand formula</a>.</li>
<li>An <a href="/wiki/Open_shop" title="Open shop">open shop</a> does not require union membership in employing or keeping workers. Where a union is active, workers who do not contribute to a union may include those who approve of the union contract (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_riders" title="Free riders">free riders</a>) and those who do not. In the United States, state level <a href="/wiki/Right-to-work_law" title="Right-to-work law">right-to-work laws</a> mandate the open shop in some states. In Germany only open shops are legal; that is, all discrimination based on union membership is forbidden. This affects the function and services of the union.</li></ul>
<p>An EU case concerning Italy stated that, "The principle of trade union freedom in the Italian system implies recognition of the right of the individual not to belong to any trade union ("negative" freedom of association/trade union freedom), and the unlawfulness of discrimination liable to cause harm to non-unionized employees."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Britain, previous to this EU jurisprudence, a series of laws introduced during the 1980s by Margaret Thatcher's government restricted closed and union shops. All agreements requiring a worker to join a union are now illegal. In the United States, the <a href="/wiki/Taft%E2%80%93Hartley_Act" title="Taft–Hartley Act">Taft–Hartley Act</a> of 1947 outlawed the closed shop.
</p><p>In 2006, the European Court of Human Rights found Danish closed-shop agreements to be in breach of Article 11 of the European Convention on Human Rights and Fundamental Freedoms. It was stressed that Denmark and <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a> were among a limited number of contracting states that continue to permit the conclusion of closed-shop agreements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diversity_of_international_unions">Diversity of international unions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Diversity of international unions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Union law varies from country to country, as does the function of unions. For example, German and Dutch unions have played a greater role in management decisions through participation in corporate boards and <a href="/wiki/Co-determination" title="Co-determination">co-determination</a> than have unions in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Moreover, in the United States, collective bargaining is most commonly undertaken by unions directly with employers, whereas in Austria, Denmark, Germany or Sweden, unions most often negotiate with employers associations.
</p><p>Concerning labour market regulation in the EU, Gold (1993)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> and Hall (1994)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> have identified three distinct systems of labour market regulation, which also influence the role that unions play:
</p>
<ul><li>"In the Continental European System of labour market regulation, the government plays an important role as there is a strong legislative core of employee rights, which provides the basis for agreements as well as a framework for discord between unions on one side and employers or employers' associations on the other. This model was said to be found in EU core countries such as Belgium, France, Germany, the Netherlands and Italy, and it is also mirrored and emulated to some extent in the institutions of the EU, due to the relative weight that these countries had in the EU until the EU expansion by the inclusion of 10 new <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern European</a> member states in 2004.</li>
<li>In the Anglo-Saxon System of labour market regulation, the government's legislative role is much more limited, which allows for more issues to be decided between employers and employees and any union or employers' associations which might represent these parties in the decision-making process. However, in these countries, collective agreements are not widespread; only a few businesses and a few sectors of the economy have a strong tradition of finding collective solutions in labour relations. <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Ireland</a> and the UK belong to this category, and in contrast to the EU core countries above, these countries first joined the EU in 1973.</li>
<li>In the Nordic System of labour market regulation, the government's legislative role is limited in the same way as in the Anglo-Saxon system. However, in contrast to the countries in the Anglo-Saxon system category, this is a much more widespread network of collective agreements, which covers most industries and most firms. This model was said to encompass Denmark, Finland, Norway and Sweden. Here, Denmark joined the EU in 1973, whereas Finland and Sweden joined in 1995."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>The United States takes a more <a href="/wiki/Laissez-faire" title="Laissez-faire">laissez-faire</a> approach, setting some minimum standards but leaving most workers' wages and benefits to collective bargaining and market forces. Thus, it comes closest to the above Anglo-Saxon model. Also, the Eastern European countries that have recently entered into the EU come closest to the Anglo-Saxon model.
</p><p>In contrast, in Germany, the relation between individual employees and employers is considered to be asymmetrical. In consequence, many working conditions are not negotiable due to a strong legal protection of individuals. However, the German flavor or works legislation has as its main objective to create a balance of power between employees organized in unions and employers organized in employers associations. This allows much wider legal boundaries for collective bargaining, compared to the narrow boundaries for individual negotiations. As a condition to obtain the legal status of a trade union, employee associations need to prove that their leverage is strong enough to serve as a counterforce in negotiations with employers. If such an employees association is competing against another union, its leverage may be questioned by unions and then evaluated in a court trial. In Germany, only very few professional associations obtained the right to negotiate salaries and working conditions for their members, notably the medical doctors association Marburger Bund and the pilots association Vereinigung Cockpit. The engineers association <a href="/wiki/Verein_Deutscher_Ingenieure" title="Verein Deutscher Ingenieure">Verein Deutscher Ingenieure</a> does not strive to act as a union, as it also represents the interests of engineering businesses.
</p><p>Beyond the classification listed above, unions' relations with political parties vary. In many countries unions are tightly bonded, or even share leadership, with a political party intended to represent the interests of the working class. Typically this is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Left-wing" title="Left-wing">left-wing</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Socialist" title="Socialist">socialist</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_democrat" title="Social democrat">social democratic</a> party, but many exceptions exist, including some of the aforementioned Christian unions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poole,_M._1986_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-Poole,_M._1986-2">[2]</a></sup> In the United States, trade unions are almost always aligned with the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party</a> with a few exceptions. For example, the <a href="/wiki/International_Brotherhood_of_Teamsters" title="International Brotherhood of Teamsters">International Brotherhood of Teamsters</a> has supported <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican Party</a> candidates on a number of occasions and the <a href="/wiki/Professional_Air_Traffic_Controllers_Organization_(1968)" title="Professional Air Traffic Controllers Organization (1968)">Professional Air Traffic Controllers Organization</a> (PATCO) endorsed <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> in 1980. In Britain trade union movement's relationship with the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a> frayed as party leadership embarked on <a href="/wiki/Privatization" title="Privatization">privatization</a> plans at odds with what unions see as the worker's interests. However, it has strengthened once more after the Labour party's election of <a href="/wiki/Ed_Miliband" title="Ed Miliband">Ed Miliband</a>, who beat his brother <a href="/wiki/David_Miliband" title="David Miliband">David Miliband</a> to become leader of the party after Ed secured the trade union votes. Additionally, in the past, there was a group known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservative_Trade_Unionists" title="Conservative Trade Unionists">Conservative Trade Unionists</a>, or CTU, formed of people who sympathized with right wing Tory policy but were Trade Unionists.
</p><p>Historically, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republic_of_Korea" title="Republic of Korea">Republic of Korea</a> has regulated collective bargaining by requiring employers to participate, but collective bargaining has only been legal if held in sessions before the <a href="/wiki/Korean_New_Year" title="Korean New Year">lunar new year</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="International_unionization">International unionization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: International unionization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The oldest global trade union organizations include the <a href="/wiki/World_Federation_of_Trade_Unions" title="World Federation of Trade Unions">World Federation of Trade Unions</a> created in 1945.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>The largest trade union federation in the world is the <a href="/wiki/Brussels" title="Brussels">Brussels</a>-based <a href="/wiki/International_Trade_Union_Confederation" title="International Trade Union Confederation">International Trade Union Confederation</a> (ITUC), created in 2006,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> which has approximately 309 affiliated organizations in 156 countries and territories, with a combined membership of 166 million. The ITUC is a federation of national trade union centres, such as the AFL-CIO in the United States and the Trades Union Congress in the United Kingdom.
</p><p>National and regional trade unions organizing in specific industry sectors or occupational groups also form <a href="/wiki/Global_union_federation" title="Global union federation">global union federations</a>, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Union_Network_International" title="Union Network International">Union Network International</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Transport_Workers_Federation" title="International Transport Workers Federation">International Transport Workers Federation</a>, the <a href="/wiki/International_Federation_of_Journalists" title="International Federation of Journalists">International Federation of Journalists</a>, the International Arts and Entertainment Alliance or <a href="/wiki/Public_Services_International" title="Public Services International">Public Services International</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact">Impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economics">Economics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Economics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The academic literature shows substantial evidence that labor unions reduce <a href="/wiki/Economic_inequality" title="Economic inequality">economic inequality</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> The economist <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stiglitz" title="Joseph Stiglitz">Joseph Stiglitz</a> has asserted that, "Strong unions have helped to reduce inequality, whereas weaker unions have made it easier for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CEO" title="CEO">CEOs</a>, sometimes working with market forces that they have helped shape, to increase it." The decline in unionization since the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_World_War" title="Second World War">Second World War</a> in the United States has been associated with a pronounced rise in <a href="/wiki/Income_inequality_in_the_United_States" title="Income inequality in the United States">income</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wealth_inequality_in_the_United_States" title="Wealth inequality in the United States">wealth inequality</a> and, since 1967, with loss of <a href="/wiki/American_middle_class" title="American middle class">middle class</a> income.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Right-to-work_laws" title="Right-to-work laws">Right-to-work laws</a> have been linked to greater economic inequality in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>Research from Norway has found that high unionization rates lead to substantial increases in firm productivity, as well as increases in workers' wages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> Research from Belgium also found productivity gains, although smaller.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> Other research in the United States has found that unions can harm profitability, employment and business growth rates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> Research from the <a href="/wiki/Anglosphere" title="Anglosphere">Anglosphere</a> indicates that unions can provide wage premiums and reduce inequality while reducing employment growth and restricting employment flexibility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United States, the outsourcing of labour to Asia, Latin America, and Africa has been partially driven by increasing costs of union partnership, which gives other countries a <a href="/wiki/Comparative_advantage" title="Comparative advantage">comparative advantage</a> in labour, making it more efficient to perform labour-intensive work there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> Trade unions have been accused of benefiting insider workers and those with secure jobs at the cost of outsider workers, consumers of the goods or services produced, and the shareholders of the unionized business.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Milton_Friedman" title="Milton Friedman">Milton Friedman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Economist" title="Economist">economist</a> and advocate of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laissez-faire_capitalism" title="Laissez-faire capitalism">laissez-faire capitalism</a>, sought to show that unionization produces higher wages (for the union members) at the expense of fewer jobs, and that, if some industries are unionized while others are not, wages will tend to decline in non-unionized industries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the United States, the weakening of unions has been linked to more favorable electoral outcomes for the Republican Party.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> Legislators in areas with high unionization rates are more responsive to the interests of the poor, whereas areas with lower unionization rates are more responsive to the interests of the rich.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> Higher unionization rates increase the likelihood of parental leave policies being adopted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Republican-controlled states are less likely to adopt more restrictive labor policies when unions are strong in the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p>Research in the United States found that American congressional representatives were more responsive to the interests of the poor in districts with higher unionization rates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> Another 2020 American study found an association between US state level adoption of parental leave legislation and labor union strength.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United States, unions have been linked to lower racial resentment among whites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> Membership in unions increases political knowledge, in particular among those with less formal education.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the United States, higher union density has been associated with lower suicide/overdose deaths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> Decreased unionization rates in the United States have been linked to an increase in occupational fatalities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zoorob_2018_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zoorob_2018-136">[136]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Union_publications">Union publications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade_union&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Union publications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Several sources of current news exist about the trade union movement in the world. These include <a href="/wiki/LabourStart" title="LabourStart">LabourStart</a> and the official website of the international trade union movement <a href="/wiki/Global_Unions" title="Global Unions">Global Unions</a>. A source of international news about unions is RadioLabour which provides daily (Monday to Friday) news reports.
</p><p><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Labor_Notes" title="Labor Notes">Labor Notes</a></i> is the largest circulation cross-union publication remaining in the United States. It reports news and analysis about union activity or problems facing the labour movement. Another source of union news is the <i>Workers Independent News</i>, a news organization providing radio articles to independent and syndicated radio shows in the United States.
</p>

<li id="cite_note-Sommer_1903_p._85-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Sommer_1903_p._85_8-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFHammurabi1903"><a href="/wiki/Hammurabi" title="Hammurabi">Hammurabi</a> (1903). <li id="cite_note-135"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-135">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFEisenberg‐GuyotMooneyHagopianBarrington2020">Eisenberg‐Guyot, Jerzy; Mooney, Stephen J.; Hagopian, Amy; Barrington, Wendy E.; Hajat, Anjum (2020). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC7293351" rel="nofollow">"Solidarity and disparity: Declining labor union density and changing racial and educational mortality inequities in the United States"</a>. <i>American Journal of Industrial Medicine</i>. <b>63</b> (3): 218–231. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1002%2Fajim.23081" rel="nofollow">10.1002/ajim.23081</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISSN_(identifier)" title="ISSN (identifier)">ISSN</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/issn/1097-0274" rel="nofollow">1097-0274</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC7293351" rel="nofollow">7293351</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/31845387" rel="nofollow">31845387</a>. <q>Results – Overall, a 10% increase in union density was associated with a 17% relative decrease in overdose/suicide mortality (95% confidence interval [CI]: 0.70, 0.98), or 5.7 lives saved per 100 000 person‐years (95% CI: −10.7, −0.7). Union density's absolute (lives‐saved) effects on overdose/suicide mortality were stronger for men than women, but its relative effects were similar across genders. Union density had little effect on all‐cause mortality overall or across subgroups, and modeling suggested union‐density increases would not affect mortality inequities. Conclusions - Declining union density (as operationalized in this study) may not explain all‐cause mortality inequities, although increases in union density may reduce overdose/suicide mortality.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=American+Journal+of+Industrial+Medicine&amp;rft.atitle=Solidarity+and+disparity%3A+Declining+labor+union+density+and+changing+racial+and+educational+mortality+inequities+in+the+United+States&amp;rft.volume=63&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.pages=218-231&amp;rft.date=2020&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC7293351%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft.issn=1097-0274&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F31845387&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1002%2Fajim.23081&amp;rft.aulast=Eisenberg%E2%80%90Guyot&amp;rft.aufirst=Jerzy&amp;rft.au=Mooney%2C+Stephen+J.&amp;rft.au=Hagopian%2C+Amy&amp;rft.au=Barrington%2C+Wendy+E.&amp;rft.au=Hajat%2C+Anjum&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC7293351&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATrade+union"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Zoorob_2018-136"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Zoorob_2018_136-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFZoorob2018">Zoorob, Michael (1 October 2018). <a class="external text" href="https://oem.bmj.com/content/75/10/736" rel="nofollow">"Does 'right to work' imperil the right to health?
